<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the original 1950s style of rock music, see <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">Rock and roll</a>. </td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2" style="border-top: solid 1px #aaa; text-align: center;"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="31" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/31px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/47px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Gnome_globe_current_event.svg/62px-Gnome_globe_current_event.svg.png 2x" width="31"/> <i><a href="/wiki/2022_in_rock_music" title="2022 in rock music">2022 in rock music</a></i></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Rock music</b> is a broad genre of <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a> that originated as "<a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a>" in the United States in the late 1940s and early 1950s, developing into a range of different styles in the mid-1960s and later, particularly in the United States and United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-studwell_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-studwell-3">[3]</a></sup> It has its roots in 1940s and 1950s rock and roll, a style that drew directly from the <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">rhythm and blues</a> genres of <a href="/wiki/African-American_music" title="African-American music">African-American music</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country music</a>. Rock also drew strongly from a number of other genres such as <a href="/wiki/Electric_blues" title="Electric blues">electric blues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a>, and incorporated influences from <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, classical, and other musical styles. For instrumentation, rock has centered on the electric guitar, usually as part of a rock group with electric bass guitar, drums, and one or more singers. Usually, rock is song-based music with a <a href="/wiki/Time_signature" title="Time signature"><span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span style="font-family: 'Georgia Pro', Georgia, 'DejaVu Serif', Times, 'Times New Roman', FreeSerif, 'DejaVu Math TeX', 'URW Bookman L', serif;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:0.85em;font-size:80%;text-align:center"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><b>4</b></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><b>4</b></sub></span></span></span> time signature</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Verse%E2%80%93chorus_form" title="Verse–chorus form">verse–chorus form</a>, but the genre has become extremely diverse. Like pop music, lyrics often stress romantic love but also address a wide variety of other themes that are frequently social or political.
</p><p>Rock musicians in the mid-1960s began to advance the album ahead of the single as the dominant form of recorded music expression and consumption, with <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a> at the forefront of this development. Their contributions lent the genre a cultural legitimacy in the mainstream and initiated a rock-informed <a href="/wiki/Album_era" title="Album era">album era</a> in the music industry for the next several decades. By the late 1960s "<a href="/wiki/Classic_rock" title="Classic rock">classic rock</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-studwell_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-studwell-3">[3]</a></sup> period, a number of distinct rock music subgenres had emerged, including hybrids like <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">blues rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">folk rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Country_rock" title="Country rock">country rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_rock" title="Southern rock">southern rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raga_rock" title="Raga rock">raga rock</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jazz_rock" title="Jazz rock">jazz rock</a>, many of which contributed to the development of <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a>, which was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">countercultural</a> <a href="/wiki/Psychedelia" title="Psychedelia">psychedelic and hippie scene</a>. New genres that emerged included <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">progressive rock</a>, which extended the artistic elements, <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">glam rock</a>, which highlighted showmanship and visual style, and the diverse and enduring subgenre of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal</a>, which emphasized volume, power, and speed. In the second half of the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> reacted by producing stripped-down, energetic social and political critiques. Punk was an influence in the 1980s on <a href="/wiki/New_wave_music" title="New wave music">new wave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Post-punk" title="Post-punk">post-punk</a> and eventually <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative rock</a>.
</p><p>From the 1990s, alternative rock began to dominate rock music and break into the mainstream in the form of <a href="/wiki/Grunge" title="Grunge">grunge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Britpop" title="Britpop">Britpop</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indie_rock" title="Indie rock">indie rock</a>. Further fusion subgenres have since emerged, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pop_punk" title="Pop punk">pop punk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electronic_rock" title="Electronic rock">electronic rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rap_rock" title="Rap rock">rap rock</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rap_metal" title="Rap metal">rap metal</a>, as well as conscious attempts to revisit rock's history, including the <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">garage rock</a>/<a href="/wiki/Post-punk_revival" title="Post-punk revival">post-punk</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Techno-pop" title="Techno-pop">techno-pop</a> revivals in the 2000s. The 2010s saw a slow decline in rock music's mainstream popularity and cultural relevancy, with <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop</a> surpassing it as the most popular genre in the United States.
</p><p>
Rock has also embodied and served as the vehicle for cultural and social movements, leading to major subcultures including <a href="/wiki/Mod_(subculture)" title="Mod (subculture)">mods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocker_(subculture)" title="Rocker (subculture)">rockers</a> in the United Kingdom and the <a href="/wiki/Hippie" title="Hippie">hippie</a> counterculture that spread out from San Francisco in the US in the 1960s. Similarly, 1970s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punk_culture" title="Punk culture">punk culture</a> spawned the <a href="/wiki/Goth_subculture" title="Goth subculture">goth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emo" title="Emo">emo</a> subcultures. Inheriting the folk tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest song</a>, rock music has been associated with political activism as well as changes in social attitudes to race, sex, and drug use, and is often seen as an expression of youth revolt against adult <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conformity" title="Conformity">conformity</a>. At the same time, it has been commercially highly successful, leading to charges of <a href="/wiki/Selling_out" title="Selling out">selling out</a>.<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style></p><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:18%; ; padding:8px;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>A good definition of rock, in fact, is that it's popular music that to a certain degree doesn't care if it's popular.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/Bill_Wyman" title="Bill Wyman">Bill Wyman</a> in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vulture.com" title="Vulture.com">Vulture</a></i> (2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wymanberry_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-wymanberry-4">[4]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg"><img alt="A photograph of four members of the Red Hot Chili Peppers performing on a stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg/220px-Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg/330px-Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg/440px-Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rhcp-live-pinkpop05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Red_Hot_Chili_Peppers" title="Red Hot Chili Peppers">Red Hot Chili Peppers</a> in 2006, showing a quartet lineup for a rock band (from left to right: bassist, lead vocalist, drummer, and guitarist)</div></div></div>
<p>The sound of rock is traditionally centered on the <a href="/wiki/Guitar_amplifier" title="Guitar amplifier">amplified</a> electric guitar, which emerged in its modern form in the 1950s with the popularity of rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Also, it was influenced by the sounds of <a href="/wiki/Electric_blues" title="Electric blues">electric blues</a> guitarists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-6">[6]</a></sup> The sound of an electric guitar in rock music is typically supported by an electric bass guitar, which pioneered in jazz music in the same era,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and percussion produced from a drum kit that combines drums and cymbals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> This trio of instruments has often been complemented by the inclusion of other instruments, particularly keyboards such as the piano, the <a href="/wiki/Hammond_organ" title="Hammond organ">Hammond organ</a>, and the synthesizer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The basic rock instrumentation was derived from the basic <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> band instrumentation (prominent lead guitar, second chordal instrument, bass, and drums).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-6">[6]</a></sup> A group of musicians performing rock music is termed as a rock band or a rock group. Furthermore, it typically consists of between three (the <a href="/wiki/Power_trio" title="Power trio">power trio</a>) and five members. Classically, a rock band takes the form of a <a href="/wiki/Quartet" title="Quartet">quartet</a> whose members cover one or more roles, including vocalist, lead guitarist, rhythm guitarist, bass guitarist, drummer, and often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keyboard_player" title="Keyboard player">keyboard player</a> or other instrumentalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Characteristic_rock_drum_pattern.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="243" drum pattern common in rock music <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Simple_duple_drum_pattern.mid" title="About class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Simple_duple_drum_pattern.mid" title="File:Simple duple drum pattern.mid"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span></div></div></div>
<p>Rock music is traditionally built on a foundation of simple syncopated rhythms in a <span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span style="font-family: 'Georgia Pro', Georgia, 'DejaVu Serif', Times, 'Times New Roman', FreeSerif, 'DejaVu Math TeX', 'URW Bookman L', serif;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:0.85em;font-size:80%;text-align:center"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><b>4</b></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"><b>4</b></sub></span></span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meter_(music)" title="Meter (music)">meter</a>, with a repetitive snare drum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Backbeat_(music)" title="Backbeat (music)">back beat</a> on beats two and four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammer2004_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ammer2004-11">[11]</a></sup> Melodies often originate from older <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_modes" title="Musical modes">musical modes</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Dorian_mode" title="Dorian mode">Dorian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixolydian" title="Mixolydian">Mixolydian</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Major_scale" title="Major scale">major</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor_mode" title="Minor mode">minor</a> modes. Harmonies range from the common <a href="/wiki/Triad_(music)" title="Triad (music)">triad</a> to parallel <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fourth" title="Perfect fourth">perfect fourths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">fifths</a> and dissonant harmonic progressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammer2004_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ammer2004-11">[11]</a></sup> Since the late 1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and particularly from the mid-1960s onwards, rock music often used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verse-chorus_form" title="Verse-chorus form">verse-chorus structure</a> derived from blues and folk music, but there has been considerable variation from this model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Critics have stressed the eclecticism and stylistic diversity of rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Because of its complex history and its tendency to borrow from other musical and cultural forms, it has been argued that "it is impossible to bind rock music to a rigidly delineated musical definition."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In the opinion of music journalist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a>, "the best rock jolts <a href="/wiki/Folk_arts" title="Folk arts">folk-art</a> virtues—directness, utility, natural audience—into the present with shots of modern technology and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist" title="Modernist">modernist</a> <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(rhetoric)" title="Dissociation (rhetoric)">dissociation</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CG2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-CG2-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:18%; ; padding:8px;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Rock and roll was conceived as an outlet for adolescent yearnings ... To make rock and roll is also an ideal way to explore <a href="/wiki/Intersectionality" title="Intersectionality">intersections</a> of sex, love, violence, and fun, to broadcast the delights and limitations of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_area" title="Cultural area">the regional</a>, and to deal with the depredations and benefits of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_culture" title="Mass culture">mass culture</a> itself.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">—<a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Christgau%27s_Record_Guide:_Rock_Albums_of_the_Seventies" title="Christgau's Record Guide: Rock Albums of the Seventies">Christgau's Record Guide</a></i> (1981)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CG_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-CG-17">[17]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Unlike many earlier styles of popular music, rock lyrics have dealt with a wide range of themes, including romantic love, sex, rebellion against "<a href="/wiki/The_Establishment" title="The Establishment">The Establishment</a>", social concerns, and life styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ammer2004_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ammer2004-11">[11]</a></sup> These themes were inherited from a variety of sources such as the <a href="/wiki/Tin_Pan_Alley" title="Tin Pan Alley">Tin Pan Alley</a> pop tradition, folk music, and rhythm and blues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Christgau characterizes rock lyrics as a "cool medium" with simple diction and repeated refrains, and asserts that rock's primary "function" "pertains to music, or, more generally, <a href="/wiki/Noise_in_music" title="Noise in music">noise</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The predominance of white, male, and often middle class musicians in rock music has often been noted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and rock has been seen as an appropriation of black musical forms for a young, white and largely male audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> As a result, it has also been seen to articulate the concerns of this group in both style and lyrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Christgau, writing in 1972, said in spite of some exceptions, "rock and roll usually implies an identification of male sexuality and aggression".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the term "rock" started being used in preference to "rock and roll" from the late-1960s, it has usually been contrasted with pop music, with which it has shared many characteristics, but from which it is often distanced by an emphasis on musicianship, live performance, and a focus on serious and progressive themes as part of an ideology of <a href="/wiki/Authenticity_(philosophy)" title="Authenticity (philosophy)">authenticity</a> that is frequently combined with an awareness of the genre's history and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-24">[24]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Simon_Frith" title="Simon Frith">Simon Frith</a>, rock was "something more than pop, something more than rock and roll" and "[r]ock musicians combined an emphasis on skill and technique with the romantic concept of art as artistic expression, original and sincere".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the new millennium, the term <i>rock</i> has occasionally been used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blanket_term" title="Blanket term">blanket term</a> including forms like pop music, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reggae_music" title="Reggae music">reggae music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul music</a>, and even <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hip_hop" title="Hip hop">hip hop</a>, which it has been influenced with but often contrasted through much of its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Christgau has used the term broadly to refer to popular and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semipopular_music" title="Semipopular music">semipopular music</a> that cater to his sensibility as "a rock-and-roller", including a fondness for a good beat, a meaningful lyric with some wit, and the theme of youth, which holds an "eternal attraction" so objective "that all youth music partakes of sociology and the <a href="/wiki/Field_research" title="Field research">field report</a>." Writing in <i><a href="/wiki/Christgau%27s_Record_Guide:_The_%2780s" title="Christgau's Record Guide: The '80s">Christgau's Record Guide: The '80s</a></i> (1990), he said this sensibility is evident in the music of folk singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Shocked" title="Michelle Shocked">Michelle Shocked</a>, rapper <a href="/wiki/LL_Cool_J" title="LL Cool J">LL Cool J</a>, and synth-pop duo <a href="/wiki/Pet_Shop_Boys" title="Pet Shop Boys">Pet Shop Boys</a>—"all kids working out their identities"—as much as it is in the music of <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ramones" title="The Ramones">the Ramones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Replacements_(band)" title="The Replacements (band)">the Replacements</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CG80s_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-CG80s-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Late_1940s.E2.80.93mid-1960s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_1940s–mid-1960s">Late 1940s–mid-1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Late 1940s–mid-1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rock_and_roll">Rock and roll</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rock and roll">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">Rock and roll</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_rock_and_roll" title="Origins of rock and roll">Origins of rock and roll</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rockabilly" title="Rockabilly">Rockabilly</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1294" data-file-width="1013" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg/170px-Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg/255px-Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg/340px-Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chuck_Berry_circa_1958.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a> in a 1958 publicity photo</div></div></div>
<p>The foundations of rock music are in rock and roll, which originated in the United States during the late 1940s and early 1950s, and quickly spread to much of the rest of the world. Its immediate origins lay in a melding of various <a href="/wiki/African-American_music" title="African-American music">black musical</a> genres of the time, including <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">rhythm and blues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">gospel music</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country and western</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BrithofR&amp;R_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BrithofR&amp;R-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1951, <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland, Ohio</a> disc jockey <a href="/wiki/Alan_Freed" title="Alan Freed">Alan Freed</a> began playing rhythm and blues music (then termed "<a href="/wiki/Race_record" title="Race record">race music</a>") for a multi-racial audience, and is credited with first using the phrase "rock and roll" to describe the music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Elvis Presley standing between two sets of bars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3448" data-file-width="2673" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg/170px-Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg/255px-Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg/340px-Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elvis_Presley_promoting_Jailhouse_Rock.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a> in a promotion shot for <i><a href="/wiki/Jailhouse_Rock_(film)" title="Jailhouse Rock (film)">Jailhouse Rock</a></i> in 1957</div></div></div>
<p>Debate surrounds the many recordings which have been suggested as "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_rock_and_roll_record" title="First rock and roll record">first rock and roll record</a>". Contenders include <a href="/wiki/Wynonie_Harris" title="Wynonie Harris">Wynonie Harris</a>' "<a href="/wiki/Good_Rocking_Tonight" title="Good Rocking Tonight">Good Rocking Tonight</a>" (1948);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Goree_Carter" title="Goree Carter">Goree Carter</a>'s "Rock Awhile" (1949);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer19_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer19-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Preston" title="Jimmy Preston">Jimmy Preston</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Rock_the_Joint" title="Rock the Joint">Rock the Joint</a>" (1949), which was later <a href="/wiki/Cover_version" title="Cover version">covered</a> by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Haley_%26_His_Comets" title="Bill Haley &amp; His Comets">Bill Haley &amp; His Comets</a> in 1952;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and "<a href="/wiki/Rocket_88" title="Rocket 88">Rocket 88</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Brenston" title="Jackie Brenston">Jackie Brenston</a> and his Delta Cats (in fact, <a href="/wiki/Ike_Turner" title="Ike Turner">Ike Turner</a> and his band the <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Rhythm" title="Kings of Rhythm">Kings of Rhythm</a>), recorded by <a href="/wiki/Sam_Phillips" title="Sam Phillips">Sam Phillips</a> for <a href="/wiki/Sun_Records" title="Sun Records">Sun Records</a> in 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2008_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2008-32">[32]</a></sup> Four years later, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bill_Haley_(musician)" title="Bill Haley (musician)">Bill Haley</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Rock_Around_the_Clock" title="Rock Around the Clock">Rock Around the Clock</a>" (1955) became the first rock and roll song to top <i><a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">Billboard</a></i> magazine's main sales and airplay charts, and opened the door worldwide for this new wave of popular culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55,_track_2_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55,_track_2-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>It also has been argued that "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/That%27s_All_Right_(Mama)" title="That's All Right (Mama)">That's All Right (Mama)</a>" (1954), <a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>'s first single for Sun Records in <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Tennessee" title="Memphis, Tennessee">Memphis</a>, could be the first rock and roll record,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> but, at the same time, <a href="/wiki/Big_Joe_Turner" title="Big Joe Turner">Big Joe Turner</a>'s "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shake,_Rattle_%26_Roll" title="Shake, Rattle &amp; Roll">Shake, Rattle &amp; Roll</a>", later covered by Haley, was already at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Hot_R%26B/Hip-Hop_Songs" title="Hot R&amp;B/Hip-Hop Songs">Billboard R&amp;B charts</a>. Other artists with early rock and roll hits included <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Berry" title="Chuck Berry">Chuck Berry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bo_Diddley" title="Bo Diddley">Bo Diddley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fats_Domino" title="Fats Domino">Fats Domino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_Richard" title="Little Richard">Little Richard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Lee_Lewis" title="Jerry Lee Lewis">Jerry Lee Lewis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gene_Vincent" title="Gene Vincent">Gene Vincent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell2008_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2008-32">[32]</a></sup> Soon rock and roll was the major force in American record sales and <a href="/wiki/Crooner" title="Crooner">crooners</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eddie_Fisher_(singer)" title="Eddie Fisher (singer)">Eddie Fisher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perry_Como" title="Perry Como">Perry Como</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Patti_Page" title="Patti Page">Patti Page</a>, who had dominated the previous decade of popular music, found their access to the pop charts significantly curtailed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rock and roll has been seen as leading to a number of distinct subgenres, including rockabilly, combining rock and roll with "hillbilly" country music, which was usually played and recorded in the mid-1950s by white singers such as <a href="/wiki/Carl_Perkins" title="Carl Perkins">Carl Perkins</a>, Jerry Lee Lewis, <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Holly" title="Buddy Holly">Buddy Holly</a> and with the greatest commercial success, Elvis Presley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicRbilly_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicRbilly-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">Hispanic and Latino American</a> movements in rock and roll, which would eventually lead to the success of <a href="/wiki/Latin_rock" title="Latin rock">Latin rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicano_rock" title="Chicano rock">Chicano rock</a> within the US, began to rise in <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">the Southwest</a>; with rock and roll standard musician <a href="/wiki/Ritchie_Valens" title="Ritchie Valens">Ritchie Valens</a> and even those within other heritage genres, such as <a href="/wiki/Al_Hurricane" title="Al Hurricane">Al Hurricane</a> along with his brothers Tiny Morrie and Baby Gaby as they began combining rock and roll with country-<a href="/wiki/Western_music_(North_America)" title="Western music (North America)">western</a> within traditional <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico_music" title="New Mexico music">New Mexico music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucero_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucero-38">[38]</a></sup> Other styles like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doo_wop" title="Doo wop">doo wop</a> placed an emphasis on multi-part vocal harmonies and backing lyrics (from which the genre later gained its name), which were usually supported with light instrumentation and had its origins in 1930s and 1940s African American vocal groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Shuker,_2005_p._35_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-R._Shuker,_2005_p._35-39">[39]</a></sup> Acts like <a href="/wiki/The_Crows" title="The Crows">the Crows</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Penguins" title="The Penguins">the Penguins</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_El_Dorados" title="The El Dorados">the El Dorados</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Turbans" title="The Turbans">the Turbans</a> all scored major hits, and groups like <a href="/wiki/The_Platters" title="The Platters">the Platters</a>, with songs including "<a href="/wiki/The_Great_Pretender" title="The Great Pretender">The Great Pretender</a>" (1955),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_5,_track_3_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_5,_track_3-40">[40]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Coasters" title="The Coasters">the Coasters</a> with humorous songs like "<a href="/wiki/Yakety_Yak" title="Yakety Yak">Yakety Yak</a>" (1958),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_13_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_13-41">[41]</a></sup> ranked among the most successful rock and roll acts of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002DooWop_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002DooWop-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The era also saw the growth in popularity of the electric guitar, and the development of a specifically rock and roll style of playing through such exponents as Chuck Berry, <a href="/wiki/Link_Wray" title="Link Wray">Link Wray</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scotty_Moore" title="Scotty Moore">Scotty Moore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">distortion</a>, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Electric_blues" title="Electric blues">electric blues</a> guitarists such as <a href="/wiki/Guitar_Slim" title="Guitar Slim">Guitar Slim</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aswellslim_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-aswellslim-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Willie_Johnson_(guitarist)" title="Willie Johnson (guitarist)">Willie Johnson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Hare" title="Pat Hare">Pat Hare</a> in the early 1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-45">[45]</a></sup> was popularized by Chuck Berry in the mid-1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Power_chord" title="Power chord">power chords</a>, pioneered by Willie Johnson and Pat Hare in the early 1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-palmer-45">[45]</a></sup> was popularized by Link Wray in the late 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Trad_jazz" title="Trad jazz">trad jazz</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_folk_music" title="English folk music">folk</a> movements brought visiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blues_music" title="Blues music">blues music</a> artists to Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz2007_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz2007-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lonnie_Donegan" title="Lonnie Donegan">Lonnie Donegan</a>'s 1955 hit "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_Island_Line_(song)" title="Rock Island Line (song)">Rock Island Line</a>" was a major influence and helped to develop the trend of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skiffle_music" title="Skiffle music">skiffle music</a> groups throughout the country, many of which, including <a href="/wiki/John_Lennon" title="John Lennon">John Lennon</a>'s <a href="/wiki/The_Quarrymen" title="The Quarrymen">Quarrymen</a>, moved on to play rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commentators have traditionally perceived a decline of rock and roll in the late 1950s and early 1960s. By 1959, the death of Buddy Holly, <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Bopper" title="The Big Bopper">the Big Bopper</a> and Ritchie Valens in a plane crash, the departure of Elvis for the army, the retirement of Little Richard to become a preacher, prosecutions of Jerry Lee Lewis and Chuck Berry and the breaking of the <a href="/wiki/Payola" title="Payola">payola</a> scandal (which implicated major figures, including Alan Freed, in bribery and corruption in promoting individual acts or songs), gave a sense that the rock and roll era established at that point had come to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pop_rock_and_instrumental_rock">Pop rock and instrumental rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pop rock and instrumental rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pop_rock" title="Pop rock">Pop rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_rock" title="Instrumental rock">Instrumental rock</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doo_Wop" title="Doo Wop">Doo Wop</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_rock_and_roll" title="British rock and roll">British rock and roll</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">Soul music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg"><img alt="A color photograph of Don and Phil Everly on stage with guitars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="730" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg/220px-Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg/330px-Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg/440px-Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Everlys_Brothers_in_concert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Everly_Brothers" title="The Everly Brothers">The Everly Brothers</a> in 2006</div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>pop</i> has been used since the early 20th century to refer to popular music in general, but from the mid-1950s it began to be used for a distinct genre, aimed at a youth market, often characterized as a softer alternative to rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firth2001_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Firth2001-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicPR_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicPR-52">[52]</a></sup> From about 1967, it was increasingly used in opposition to the term rock music, to describe a form that was more commercial, ephemeral and accessible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-24">[24]</a></sup> In contrast rock music was seen as focusing on extended works, particularly albums, was often associated with particular sub-cultures (like the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a>), placed an emphasis on artistic values and "authenticity", stressed live performance and instrumental or vocal virtuosity and was often seen as encapsulating progressive developments rather than simply reflecting existing trends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warner2003_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-Warner2003-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Firth2001_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Firth2001-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicPR_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicPR-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Nevertheless, much pop and rock music has been very similar in sound, instrumentation and even lyrical content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[nb 1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The period of the later 1950s and early 1960s has traditionally been seen as an era of hiatus for rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969shows_20–21_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969shows_20–21-58">[57]</a></sup> More recently some authors<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (February 2017)">weasel words</span></a></i>]</sup> have emphasised important innovations and trends in this period without which future developments would not have been possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keightley2001_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keightley2001-60">[59]</a></sup> While early rock and roll, particularly through the advent of rockabilly, saw the greatest commercial success for male and white performers, in this era, the genre was dominated by black and female artists. Rock and roll had not disappeared at the end of the 1950s and some of its energy can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Twist_(dance)" title="Twist (dance)">Twist</a> dance craze of the early 1960s, mainly benefiting the career of <a href="/wiki/Chubby_Checker" title="Chubby Checker">Chubby Checker</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keightley2001_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keightley2001-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[nb 2]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="893" data-file-width="1252" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg/220px-James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg/330px-James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg/440px-James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Brown_Music_Scene_1969.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Brown" title="James Brown">James Brown</a> performing in 1969</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cliff_Richard" title="Cliff Richard">Cliff Richard</a> had the first <a href="/wiki/British_rock_and_roll" title="British rock and roll">British rock and roll</a> hit with "<a href="/wiki/Move_It" title="Move It">Move It</a>", effectively ushering in the sound of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_rock" title="British rock">British rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup> At the start of the 1960s, his backing group <a href="/wiki/The_Shadows" title="The Shadows">the Shadows</a> was the most successful group recording instrumentals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup> While rock and roll was fading into lightweight pop and ballads, British rock groups at clubs and local dances, heavily influenced by blues-rock pioneers like <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Korner" title="Alexis Korner">Alexis Korner</a>, were starting to play with an intensity and drive seldom found in white American acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also significant was the advent of <a href="/wiki/Soul_music" title="Soul music">soul music</a> as a major commercial force. Developing out of rhythm and blues with a re-injection of gospel music and pop, led by pioneers like <a href="/wiki/Ray_Charles" title="Ray Charles">Ray Charles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sam_Cooke" title="Sam Cooke">Sam Cooke</a> from the mid-1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55,_track_3;_shows_15–17_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_55,_track_3;_shows_15–17-67">[65]</a></sup> by the early 1960s figures like <a href="/wiki/Marvin_Gaye" title="Marvin Gaye">Marvin Gaye</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Brown" title="James Brown">James Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aretha_Franklin" title="Aretha Franklin">Aretha Franklin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curtis_Mayfield" title="Curtis Mayfield">Curtis Mayfield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder" title="Stevie Wonder">Stevie Wonder</a> were dominating the R&amp;B charts and breaking through into the main pop charts, helping to accelerate their desegregation, while <a href="/wiki/Motown" title="Motown">Motown</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stax/Volt" title="Stax/Volt">Stax/Volt</a> Records were becoming major forces in the record industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Soul_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Soul-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[nb 3]</a></sup> Some music historians have also pointed to important and innovative technical developments that built on rock and roll in this period, including the electronic treatment of sound by such innovators as <a href="/wiki/Joe_Meek" title="Joe Meek">Joe Meek</a>, and the elaborate production methods of the <a href="/wiki/Wall_of_Sound" title="Wall of Sound">Wall of Sound</a> pursued by <a href="/wiki/Phil_Spector" title="Phil Spector">Phil Spector</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keightley2001_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Keightley2001-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surf_music">Surf music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Surf music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Surf_music" title="Surf music">Surf music</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="817" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/220px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/330px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg/440px-Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sullivan_Beach_Boys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">The Beach Boys</a> performing in 1964</div></div></div>
<p>The instrumental rock and roll of performers such as <a href="/wiki/Duane_Eddy" title="Duane Eddy">Duane Eddy</a>, Link Wray and <a href="/wiki/The_Ventures" title="The Ventures">the Ventures</a> was developed by <a href="/wiki/Dick_Dale" title="Dick Dale">Dick Dale</a>, who added distinctive "wet" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reverb" title="Reverb">reverb</a>, rapid alternate picking, and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_music" title="Middle Eastern music">Middle Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Mexico" title="Music of Mexico">Mexican</a> influences. He produced the regional hit "<a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Go_Trippin%27" title="Let's Go Trippin'">Let's Go Trippin'</a>" in 1961 and launched the surf music craze, following up with songs like "<a href="/wiki/Misirlou" title="Misirlou">Misirlou</a>" (1962).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blair2_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-blair2-71">[68]</a></sup> Like Dale and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Del-Tones" title="Del-Tones">Del-Tones</a>, most early surf bands were formed in Southern California, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bel-Airs" title="Bel-Airs">Bel-Airs</a>, the <a href="/wiki/The_Challengers_(band)" title="The Challengers (band)">Challengers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eddie_%26_the_Showmen" title="Eddie &amp; the Showmen">Eddie &amp; the Showmen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blair2_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-blair2-71">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Chantays" title="The Chantays">The Chantays</a> scored a top ten national hit with "<a href="/wiki/Pipeline_(instrumental)" title="Pipeline (instrumental)">Pipeline</a>" in 1963 and probably the best known surf tune was 1963's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wipe_Out_(song)" title="Wipe Out (song)">Wipe Out</a>", by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surfaris" title="Surfaris">Surfaris</a>, which hit number 2 and number 10 on the <i>Billboard</i> charts in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Surf music achieved its greatest commercial success as vocal music, particularly the work of <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">the Beach Boys</a>, formed in 1961 in Southern California. Their early albums included both instrumental surf rock (among them covers of music by Dick Dale) and vocal songs, drawing on rock and roll and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doo_wop" title="Doo wop">doo wop</a> and the close harmonies of vocal pop acts like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_Freshmen" title="Four Freshmen">Four Freshmen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup> The Beach Boys first chart hit, "<a href="/wiki/Surfin%27" title="Surfin'">Surfin'</a>" in 1962 reached the <i>Billboard</i> top 100 and helped make the surf music craze a national phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Beachboys_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Beachboys-74">[71]</a></sup> It is often argued that the surf music craze and the careers of almost all surf acts was effectively ended by the arrival of the British Invasion from 1964, because most surf music hits were recorded and released between 1961 and 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[nb 4]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span id="Mid-1960s.E2.80.93early_1990s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-1960s–early_1990s">Mid-1960s–early 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mid-1960s–early 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_Invasion">British Invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: British Invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">Beat music</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_rock" title="British rock">British rock</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Beatles_in_America.JPG"><img alt="Black and white picture of the Beatles waving in front of a crowd with an set of aeroplane steps in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2526" data-file-width="3578" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/The_Beatles_in_America.JPG/220px-The_Beatles_in_America.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/The_Beatles_in_America.JPG/330px-The_Beatles_in_America.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/The_Beatles_in_America.JPG/440px-The_Beatles_in_America.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Beatles_in_America.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> arriving in New York at the start of the <a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a>, January 1964</div></div></div>
<p>By the end of 1962, what would become the British rock scene had started with <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">beat groups</a> like <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerry_%26_the_Pacemakers" title="Gerry &amp; the Pacemakers">Gerry &amp; the Pacemakers</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Searchers_(band)" title="The Searchers (band)">the Searchers</a> from Liverpool and <a href="/wiki/Freddie_and_the_Dreamers" title="Freddie and the Dreamers">Freddie and the Dreamers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herman%27s_Hermits" title="Herman's Hermits">Herman's Hermits</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Hollies" title="The Hollies">the Hollies</a> from Manchester. They drew on a wide range of American influences including 1950s rock and roll, soul, rhythm and blues, and surf music,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stakes2001_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stakes2001-77">[73]</a></sup> initially reinterpreting standard American tunes and playing for dancers. Bands like <a href="/wiki/The_Animals" title="The Animals">the Animals</a> from <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Them_(band)" title="Them (band)">Them</a> from <a href="/wiki/Belfast" title="Belfast">Belfast</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup> and particularly those from London like <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">the Rolling Stones</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Yardbirds" title="The Yardbirds">the Yardbirds</a>, were much more directly influenced by rhythm and blues and later blues music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup> Soon these groups were composing their own material, combining US forms of music and infusing it with a high energy beat. Beat bands tended towards "bouncy, irresistible melodies", while early <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a> acts tended towards less sexually innocent, more aggressive songs, often adopting an anti-establishment stance. There was, however, particularly in the early stages, considerable musical crossover between the two tendencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BI_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BI-80">[76]</a></sup> By 1963, led by the Beatles, beat groups had begun to achieve national success in Britain, soon to be followed into the charts by the more rhythm and blues focused acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BritishR&amp;B_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BritishR&amp;B-81">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/I_Want_to_Hold_Your_Hand" title="I Want to Hold Your Hand">I Want to Hold Your Hand</a>" was the Beatles' first number one hit on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_28_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_28-82">[78]</a></sup> spending seven weeks at the top and a total of 15 weeks on the chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaBI_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaBI-83">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup> Their first appearance on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ed_Sullivan_Show" title="The Ed Sullivan Show">The Ed Sullivan Show</a></i> on 9 February 1964, drawing an estimated 73 million viewers (at the time a record for an American television program) is considered a milestone in American pop culture. During the week of 4 April 1964, the Beatles held 12 positions on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> singles chart, including the entire top five. The Beatles went on to become the biggest selling rock band of all time and they were followed into the US charts by numerous British bands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002BI_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002BI-80">[76]</a></sup> During the next two years British acts dominated their own and the US charts with <a href="/wiki/Peter_and_Gordon" title="Peter and Gordon">Peter and Gordon</a>, the Animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29-85">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manfred_Mann" title="Manfred Mann">Manfred Mann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petula_Clark" title="Petula Clark">Petula Clark</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_29-85">[81]</a></sup> Freddie and the Dreamers, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wayne_Fontana_and_the_Mindbenders" title="Wayne Fontana and the Mindbenders">Wayne Fontana and the Mindbenders</a>, Herman's Hermits, the Rolling Stones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_30_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_30-86">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Troggs" title="The Troggs">the Troggs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Donovan" title="Donovan">Donovan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_48_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_48-87">[83]</a></sup> all having one or more number one singles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaBI_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaBI-83">[79]</a></sup> Other major acts that were part of the invasion included <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">the Kinks</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Dave_Clark_Five" title="The Dave Clark Five">the Dave Clark Five</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeatlesArrive_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-BeatlesArrive-88">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusicBI_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-allmusicBI-89">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The British Invasion helped internationalize the production of rock and roll, opening the door for subsequent British (and Irish) performers to achieve international success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmusicBritpop_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-allmusicBritpop-90">[86]</a></sup> In America it arguably spelled the end of instrumental surf music, vocal girl groups and (for a time) the <a href="/wiki/Teen_idol" title="Teen idol">teen idols</a>, that had dominated the American charts in the late 1950s and 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup> It dented the careers of established R&amp;B acts like <a href="/wiki/Fats_Domino" title="Fats Domino">Fats Domino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chubby_Checker" title="Chubby Checker">Chubby Checker</a> and even temporarily derailed the chart success of surviving rock and roll acts, including Elvis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup> The British Invasion also played a major part in the rise of a distinct genre of rock music, and cemented the primacy of the rock group, based on guitars and drums and producing their own material as singer-songwriters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R._Shuker,_2005_p._35_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-R._Shuker,_2005_p._35-39">[39]</a></sup> Following the example set by <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>' 1965 LP <i><a href="/wiki/Rubber_Soul" title="Rubber Soul">Rubber Soul</a></i> in particular, other British rock acts released rock albums intended as artistic statements in 1966, including <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">the Rolling Stones</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Aftermath_(Rolling_Stones_album)" title="Aftermath (Rolling Stones album)">Aftermath</a></i>, the Beatles' own <i><a href="/wiki/Revolver_(Beatles_album)" title="Revolver (Beatles album)">Revolver</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">the Who</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/A_Quick_One" title="A Quick One">A Quick One</a></i>, as well as American acts in <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">the Beach Boys</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Pet_Sounds" title="Pet Sounds">Pet Sounds</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Blonde_on_Blonde" title="Blonde on Blonde">Blonde on Blonde</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simonelli_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simonelli-93">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Garage_rock">Garage rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Garage rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">Garage rock</a></div>
<p>Garage rock was a raw form of rock music, particularly prevalent in North America in the mid-1960s and so called because of the perception that it was rehearsed in the suburban family garage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuker2005_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shuker2005-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup> Garage rock songs often revolved around the traumas of high school life, with songs about "lying girls" and unfair social circumstances being particularly common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-96">[92]</a></sup> The lyrics and delivery tended to be more aggressive than was common at the time, often with growled or shouted vocals that dissolved into incoherent screaming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuker2005_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shuker2005-94">[90]</a></sup> They ranged from crude one-chord music (like <a href="/wiki/The_Seeds" title="The Seeds">the Seeds</a>) to near-studio musician quality (including <a href="/wiki/The_Knickerbockers" title="The Knickerbockers">the Knickerbockers</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Remains_(band)" title="The Remains (band)">the Remains</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Estate_(band)" title="The Fifth Estate (band)">the Fifth Estate</a>). There were also regional variations in many parts of the country with flourishing scenes particularly in California and Texas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-96">[92]</a></sup> The Pacific Northwest states of Washington and Oregon had perhaps<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2017)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> the most defined regional sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_D-Men_1964.JPG"><img alt="A tinted photograph of five members of the D-Men performing with guitars, drums, and keyboards" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1480" data-file-width="1891" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/The_D-Men_1964.JPG/220px-The_D-Men_1964.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/The_D-Men_1964.JPG/330px-The_D-Men_1964.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/The_D-Men_1964.JPG/440px-The_D-Men_1964.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_D-Men_1964.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_D-Men" title="The D-Men">The D-Men</a> (later <a href="/wiki/The_Fifth_Estate_(band)" title="The Fifth Estate (band)">the Fifth Estate</a>) in 1964</div></div></div>
<p>The style had been evolving from regional scenes as early as 1958. "Tall Cool One" (1959) by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Wailers_(rock_band)" title="The Wailers (rock band)">the Wailers</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Louie_Louie" title="Louie Louie">Louie Louie</a>" by <a href="/wiki/The_Kingsmen" title="The Kingsmen">the Kingsmen</a> (1963) are mainstream examples of the genre in its formative stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup> By 1963, garage band singles were creeping into the national charts in greater numbers, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Revere_and_the_Raiders" title="Paul Revere and the Raiders">Paul Revere and the Raiders</a> (Boise),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Trashmen" title="The Trashmen">the Trashmen</a> (Minneapolis)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Rivieras" title="The Rivieras">the Rivieras</a> (South Bend, Indiana).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup> Other influential garage bands, such as <a href="/wiki/The_Sonics" title="The Sonics">the Sonics</a> (Tacoma, Washington), never reached the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The British Invasion greatly influenced garage bands, providing them with a national audience, leading many (often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surf_rock" title="Surf rock">surf</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hot_rod" title="Hot rod">hot rod</a> groups) to adopt a British influence, and encouraging many more groups to form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-96">[92]</a></sup> Thousands of garage bands were extant in the US and Canada during the era and hundreds produced regional hits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-96">[92]</a></sup> Despite scores of bands being signed to major or large regional labels, most were commercial failures. It is generally agreed that garage rock peaked both commercially and artistically around 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-96">[92]</a></sup> By 1968 the style largely disappeared from the national charts and at the local level as amateur musicians faced college, work or the <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">draft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-96">[92]</a></sup> New styles had evolved to replace garage rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllmusicGarage_96-6"><a href="#cite_note-AllmusicGarage-96">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[nb 5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blues_rock">Blues rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Blues rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">Blues rock</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a></div>
<p>Although the first impact of the <a href="/wiki/British_Invasion" title="British Invasion">British Invasion</a> on American popular music was through beat and R&amp;B based acts, the impetus was soon taken up by a second wave of bands that drew their inspiration more directly from American <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a>, including <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">the Rolling Stones</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Yardbirds" title="The Yardbirds">the Yardbirds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup> British blues musicians of the late 1950s and early 1960s had been inspired by the acoustic playing of figures such as <a href="/wiki/Lead_Belly" title="Lead Belly">Lead Belly</a>, who was a major influence on the Skiffle craze, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Johnson_(musician)" title="Robert Johnson (musician)">Robert Johnson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup> Increasingly they adopted a loud amplified sound, often centered on the electric guitar, based on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_blues" title="Chicago blues">Chicago blues</a>, particularly after the tour of Britain by <a href="/wiki/Muddy_Waters" title="Muddy Waters">Muddy Waters</a> in 1958, which prompted <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Davies" title="Cyril Davies">Cyril Davies</a> and guitarist <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Korner" title="Alexis Korner">Alexis Korner</a> to form the band <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blues_Incorporated" title="Blues Incorporated">Blues Incorporated</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-107">[102]</a></sup> The band involved and inspired many of the figures of the subsequent <a href="/wiki/British_blues" title="British blues">British blues</a> boom, including members of the Rolling Stones and <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a>, combining blues standards and forms with rock instrumentation and emphasis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BritishBlues-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg"><img alt="A black and white photograph of Eric Clapton with a guitar on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1898" data-file-width="2362" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg/220px-Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg/330px-Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg/440px-Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eric_%22slowhand%22_Clapton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a> performing in Barcelona in 1974</div></div></div>
<p>The other key focus for British blues was <a href="/wiki/John_Mayall" title="John Mayall">John Mayall</a>; his band, <a href="/wiki/John_Mayall_%26_the_Bluesbreakers" title="John Mayall &amp; the Bluesbreakers">the Bluesbreakers</a>, included <a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">Eric Clapton</a> (after Clapton's departure from the Yardbirds) and later <a href="/wiki/Peter_Green_(musician)" title="Peter Green (musician)">Peter Green</a>. Particularly significant was the release of <i><a href="/wiki/Blues_Breakers_with_Eric_Clapton" title="Blues Breakers with Eric Clapton">Blues Breakers with Eric Clapton</a> (Beano)</i> album (1966), considered one of the seminal British blues recordings and the sound of which was much emulated in both Britain and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup> Eric Clapton went on to form supergroups Cream, <a href="/wiki/Blind_Faith" title="Blind Faith">Blind Faith</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Derek_and_the_Dominos" title="Derek and the Dominos">Derek and the Dominos</a>, followed by an extensive solo career that helped bring blues rock into the mainstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-107">[102]</a></sup> Green, along with the Bluesbreaker's rhythm section <a href="/wiki/Mick_Fleetwood" title="Mick Fleetwood">Mick Fleetwood</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_McVie" title="John McVie">John McVie</a>, formed Peter Green's <a href="/wiki/Fleetwood_Mac" title="Fleetwood Mac">Fleetwood Mac</a>, who enjoyed some of the greatest commercial success in the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-107">[102]</a></sup> In the late 1960s <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Beck" title="Jeff Beck">Jeff Beck</a>, also an alumnus of the Yardbirds, moved blues rock in the direction of heavy rock with his band, <a href="/wiki/The_Jeff_Beck_Group" title="The Jeff Beck Group">the Jeff Beck Group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_107-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-107">[102]</a></sup> The last Yardbirds guitarist was <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Page" title="Jimmy Page">Jimmy Page</a>, who went on to form <i>The New Yardbirds</i> which rapidly became <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>. Many of the songs on their first three albums, and occasionally later in their careers, were expansions on traditional blues songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2003BluesRock_107-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2003BluesRock-107">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In America, blues rock had been pioneered in the early 1960s by guitarist <a href="/wiki/Lonnie_Mack" title="Lonnie Mack">Lonnie Mack</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup> but the genre began to take off in the mid-1960s as acts developed a sound similar to British blues musicians. Key acts included <a href="/wiki/Paul_Butterfield" title="Paul Butterfield">Paul Butterfield</a> (whose band acted like Mayall's Bluesbreakers in Britain as a starting point for many successful musicians), <a href="/wiki/Canned_Heat" title="Canned Heat">Canned Heat</a>, the early <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Airplane" title="Jefferson Airplane">Jefferson Airplane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janis_Joplin" title="Janis Joplin">Janis Joplin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Winter" title="Johnny Winter">Johnny Winter</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_J._Geils_Band" title="The J. Geils Band">the J.
