</p><p>Post-Britpop bands have been seen as presenting the image of the rock star as an ordinary person and their increasingly melodic music was criticised for being bland or derivative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[273]</a></sup> Post-Britpop bands like <a href="/wiki/Travis_(band)" title="Travis (band)">Travis</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Who" title="The Man Who">The Man Who</a></i> (1999), <a href="/wiki/Stereophonics" title="Stereophonics">Stereophonics</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Performance_and_Cocktails" title="Performance and Cocktails">Performance and Cocktails</a></i> (1999), <a href="/wiki/Feeder_(band)" title="Feeder (band)">Feeder</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Echo_Park_(album)" title="Echo Park (album)">Echo Park</a></i> (2001), and particularly <a href="/wiki/Coldplay" title="Coldplay">Coldplay</a> from their debut album <i><a href="/wiki/Parachutes_(Coldplay_album)" title="Parachutes (Coldplay album)">Parachutes</a></i> (2000), achieved much wider international success than most of the Britpop groups that had preceded them, and were some of the most commercially successful acts of the late 1990s and early 2000s, arguably providing a launchpad for the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">garage rock</a> or <a href="/wiki/Post-punk_revival" title="Post-punk revival">post-punk revival</a>, which has also been seen as a reaction to their introspective brand of rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicTravis_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicTravis-274">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicStereophonics_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicStereophonics-280">[275]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicColdplay_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicColdplay-281">[276]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-hardcore_and_emo">Post-hardcore and emo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Post-hardcore and emo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Post-hardcore" title="Post-hardcore">Post-hardcore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emo" title="Emo">Emo</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Screamo" title="Screamo">Screamo</a></div>
<p>Post-hardcore developed in the US, particularly in the Chicago and Washington, DC areas, in the early to mid-1980s, with bands that were inspired by the do-it-yourself ethics and guitar-heavy music of hardcore punk, but influenced by post-punk, adopting longer song formats, more complex musical structures and sometimes more melodic vocal styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMPost-Hardcore_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMPost-Hardcore-282">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>Emo also emerged from the hardcore scene in 1980s Washington, D.C., initially as "emocore", used as a term to describe bands who favored expressive vocals over the more common abrasive, barking style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-283">[278]</a></sup> The early emo scene operated as an underground, with short-lived bands releasing small-run vinyl records on tiny independent labels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AllMusicEmo_283-1"><a href="#cite_note-AllMusicEmo-283">[278]</a></sup> Emo broke into mainstream culture in the early 2000s with the platinum-selling success of Jimmy Eat World's <i><a href="/wiki/Bleed_American" title="Bleed American">Bleed American</a></i> (2001) and <a href="/wiki/Dashboard_Confessional" title="Dashboard Confessional">Dashboard Confessional</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Places_You_Have_Come_to_Fear_the_Most" title="The Places You Have Come to Fear the Most">The Places You Have Come to Fear the Most</a></i> (2003).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeRogatis2003_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeRogatis2003-284">[279]</a></sup> The new emo had a much more mainstream sound than in the 1990s and a far greater appeal amongst adolescents than its earlier incarnations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeRogatis2003_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeRogatis2003-284">[279]</a></sup> At the same time, use of the term emo expanded beyond the musical genre, becoming associated with fashion, a hairstyle and any music that expressed emotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[280]</a></sup> By 2003 post-hardcore bands had also caught the attention of major labels and began to enjoy mainstream success in the album charts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A number of these bands were seen as a more aggressive offshoot of emo and given the often vague label of <a href="/wiki/Screamo" title="Screamo">screamo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMScreamo_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMScreamo-286">[281]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Garage_rock.2Fpost-punk_revival"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Garage_rock/post-punk_revival">Garage rock/post-punk revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Garage rock/post-punk revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garage_rock_revival" title="Garage rock revival">Garage rock revival</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-punk_revival" title="Post-punk revival">Post-punk revival</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheStrokes.jpg"><img alt="a color photograph of members of the group the Strokes performing on stage" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="1429" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/TheStrokes.jpg/280px-TheStrokes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/TheStrokes.jpg/420px-TheStrokes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/TheStrokes.jpg/560px-TheStrokes.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheStrokes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Strokes" title="The Strokes">The Strokes</a> performing in 2006</div></div></div>
<p>In the early 2000s, a new group of bands that played a stripped down and back-to-basics version of guitar rock, emerged into the mainstream. They were variously characterised as part of a garage rock, post-punk or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Wave_revival" title="New Wave revival">New Wave revival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[283]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[285]</a></sup> Because the bands came from across the globe, cited diverse influences (from traditional blues, through New Wave to grunge), and adopted differing styles of dress, their unity as a genre has been disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[286]</a></sup> There had been attempts to revive garage rock and elements of punk in the 1980s and 1990s and by 2000 scenes had grown up in several countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p>The commercial breakthrough from these scenes was led by four bands: <a href="/wiki/The_Strokes" title="The Strokes">the Strokes</a>, who emerged from the New York club scene with their début album <i><a href="/wiki/Is_This_It" title="Is This It">Is This It</a></i> (2001); <a href="/wiki/The_White_Stripes" title="The White Stripes">the White Stripes</a>, from Detroit, with their third album <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Blood_Cells_(album)" title="White Blood Cells (album)">White Blood Cells</a></i> (2001); <a href="/wiki/The_Hives" title="The Hives">the Hives</a> from Sweden after their compilation album <i><a href="/wiki/Your_New_Favourite_Band" title="Your New Favourite Band">Your New Favourite Band</a></i> (2001); and <a href="/wiki/The_Vines_(band)" title="The Vines (band)">the Vines</a> from Australia with <i><a href="/wiki/Highly_Evolved" title="Highly Evolved">Highly Evolved</a></i> (2002).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[288]</a></sup> They were christened by the media as the "The" bands, and dubbed "The saviours of rock 'n' roll", leading to accusations of hype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[289]</a></sup> A second wave of bands that gained international recognition due to the movement included <a href="/wiki/Black_Rebel_Motorcycle_Club" title="Black Rebel Motorcycle Club">Black Rebel Motorcycle Club</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Killers" title="The Killers">the Killers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interpol_(band)" title="Interpol (band)">Interpol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Leon" title="Kings of Leon">Kings of Leon</a> from the US,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[290]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Libertines" title="The Libertines">the Libertines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Monkeys" title="Arctic Monkeys">Arctic Monkeys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloc_Party" title="Bloc Party">Bloc Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaiser_Chiefs" title="Kaiser Chiefs">Kaiser Chiefs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Ferdinand_(band)" title="Franz Ferdinand (band)">Franz Ferdinand</a> from the UK,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[291]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jet_(band)" title="Jet (band)">Jet</a> from Australia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[292]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Datsuns" title="The Datsuns">the Datsuns</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_D4" title="The D4">the D4</a> from New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[293]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Digital_electronic_rock">Digital electronic rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Digital electronic rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electronic_rock" title="Electronic rock">Electronic rock</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laptronica" title="Laptronica">Laptronica</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indietronica" title="Indietronica">Indietronica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electroclash" title="Electroclash">Electroclash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dance-punk" title="Dance-punk">Dance-punk</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_rave" title="New rave">New rave</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Synth-pop" title="Synth-pop">Synth-pop</a></div>
<p>In the 2000s, as computer technology became more accessible and <a href="/wiki/Music_software" title="Music software">music software</a> advanced, it became possible to create high quality music using little more than a single laptop computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S._Emmerson,_2007_pp._80-1_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-S._Emmerson,_2007_pp._80-1-299">[294]</a></sup> This resulted in a massive increase in the amount of home-produced electronic music available to the general public via the expanding internet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[295]</a></sup> and new forms of performance such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laptronica" title="Laptronica">laptronica</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S._Emmerson,_2007_pp._80-1_299-1"><a href="#cite_note-S._Emmerson,_2007_pp._80-1-299">[294]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Live_coding" title="Live coding">live coding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[296]</a></sup> These techniques also began to be used by existing bands and by developing genres that mixed rock with digital techniques and sounds, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indie_electronic" title="Indie electronic">indie electronic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electroclash" title="Electroclash">electroclash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dance-punk" title="Dance-punk">dance-punk</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_rave" title="New rave">new rave</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="2010s.E2.80.932020s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s–2020s">2010s–2020s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: 2010s–2020s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_in_mainstream_relevancy">Decline in mainstream relevancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Decline in mainstream relevancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 2010s, rock music saw a decline in mainstream popularity and cultural relevancy and by 2017, <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">hip hop music</a> had surpassed it as the most consumed musical genre in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[297]</a></sup> Critics in the latter half of the decade took notice of the genre's waning popularity, citing the popularity of hip hop<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geniuship_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geniuship-303">[298]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electronic_dance_music" title="Electronic dance music">electronic dance music</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kdot_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-kdot-304">[299]</a></sup> the rise of streaming and the advent of technology which has changed approaches toward music creation as being factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[300]</a></sup> Ken Partridge of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genius_(website)" title="Genius (website)">Genius</a> suggested that hip-hop became more popular because it is a more transformative genre and does not need to rely on past sounds and that there is a direct connection to the decline of rock music and changing social attitudes during the 2010s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geniuship_303-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geniuship-303">[298]</a></sup> Bill Flanagan, in a 2016 opinion piece for <i>The New York Times</i>, compared the state of rock during this period to the state of jazz in the early 1980s, "slowing down and looking back."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[301]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Vice_Media" title="Vice Media">Vice</a></i> suggests that this decline in popularity could actually benefit the genre by attracting outsiders with "something to prove and nothing to gain."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[302]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite rock's decline in mainstream popularity, some rock bands and groups have continued to achieve mainstream success in the 2010s and 2020s, including <a href="/wiki/Tool_(band)" title="Tool (band)">Tool</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[303]</a></sup> Fall Out Boy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kdot_304-1"><a href="#cite_note-kdot-304">[299]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greta_Van_Fleet" title="Greta Van Fleet">Greta Van Fleet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[304]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Panic!_at_the_Disco" title="Panic! at the Disco">Panic! at the Disco</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboarddecadeend_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billboarddecadeend-310">[305]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Twenty_One_Pilots" title="Twenty One Pilots">Twenty One Pilots</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboarddecadeend_310-1"><a href="#cite_note-Billboarddecadeend-310">[305]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walk_the_Moon" title="Walk the Moon">Walk the Moon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboarddecadeend_310-2"><a href="#cite_note-Billboarddecadeend-310">[305]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Portugal._The_Man" title="Portugal. The Man">Portugal. The Man</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboarddecadeend_310-3"><a href="#cite_note-Billboarddecadeend-310">[305]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Black_Keys" title="The Black Keys">the Black Keys</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billboarddecadeend_310-4"><a href="#cite_note-Billboarddecadeend-310">[305]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Avenged_Sevenfold" title="Avenged Sevenfold">Avenged Sevenfold</a>.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[306]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dreamcatcher_(group)" title="Dreamcatcher (group)">Dreamcatcher</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[307]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Foo_Fighters" title="Foo Fighters">Foo Fighters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times-313">[308]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_the_COVID-19_pandemic">Effects of the COVID-19 pandemic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Effects of the COVID-19 pandemic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Amidst the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>, the virus brought extreme changes to the rock scene worldwide. Restrictions, such as <a href="/wiki/Quarantine" title="Quarantine">quarantine</a> rules, caused widespread cancellations and postponements of concerts, tours, festivals, album releases, award ceremonies, and competitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[310]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[312]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[313]</a></sup> Some artists resorted to giving online performances to keep their careers active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[314]</a></sup> Another scheme to circumvent the quarantine limitations was used at a concert of Danish rock musician <a href="/wiki/Mads_Langer" title="Mads Langer">Mads Langer</a>: the audience watched the performance from inside their cars, much like in a <a href="/wiki/Drive-in_theater" title="Drive-in theater">drive-in theater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[315]</a></sup> Musically, the pandemic led to a surge in new releases from the slower, less energetic, and more acoustic subgenres of rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[317]</a></sup> The industry raised funds to help itself through efforts such as Crew Nation, a relief fund for live music crews organised by <a href="/wiki/Live_Nation_Entertainment" title="Live Nation Entertainment">Livenation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[318]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pop_punk_and_post-punk_revivals">Pop punk and post-punk revivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Pop punk and post-punk revivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the start of the 2020s, recording artists in both pop and rap music released popular pop-punk recordings, many of them produced or assisted by Blink-182 drummer <a href="/wiki/Travis_Barker" title="Travis Barker">Travis Barker</a>. Representing a commercial resurgence for the genre, these acts included <a href="/wiki/Machine_Gun_Kelly_(musician)" title="Machine Gun Kelly (musician)">Machine Gun Kelly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willow_Smith" title="Willow Smith">Willow Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trippie_Redd" title="Trippie Redd">Trippie Redd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halsey_(singer)" title="Halsey (singer)">Halsey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yungblud" title="Yungblud">Yungblud</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Rodrigo" title="Olivia Rodrigo">Olivia Rodrigo</a>. The popularity of the social media platform <a href="/wiki/TikTok" title="TikTok">TikTok</a> helped spark nostalgia for the angst-driven musical style among young listeners during the pandemic. Among the most successful of these releases have been Machine Gun Kelly's 2020 album <i><a href="/wiki/Tickets_to_My_Downfall" title="Tickets to My Downfall">Tickets to My Downfall</a></i>, which topped the <i>Billboard</i> 200, and Rodrigo's number-one hit single "<a href="/wiki/Good_4_U" title="Good 4 U">Good 4 U</a>" (2021).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[319]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-to-late 2010s and early 2020s, a new wave of post-punk bands from Britain and Ireland emerged. The groups in this scene have been described with the term "Crank Wave" by <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/The_Quietus" title="The Quietus">The Quietus</a> in 2019, and as "Post-<a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> New Wave" by <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Fluxblog" title="Fluxblog">Matthew Perpetua</a> in 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmemark_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-nmemark-325">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quietus1_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-quietus1-326">[321]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perpetua_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-perpetua-327">[322]</a></sup> Artists that have been identified as part of the style include <a href="/wiki/Black_Midi" title="Black Midi">Black Midi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid_(band)" title="Squid (band)">Squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Country,_New_Road" title="Black Country, New Road">Black Country, New Road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dry_Cleaning_(band)" title="Dry Cleaning (band)">Dry Cleaning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shame_(band)" title="Shame (band)">Shame</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Mods" title="Sleaford Mods">Sleaford Mods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fontaines_D.C." title="Fontaines D.C.">Fontaines D.C.</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Murder_Capital" title="The Murder Capital">The Murder Capital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idles" title="Idles">Idles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yard_Act" title="Yard Act">Yard Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmemark_325-1"><a href="#cite_note-nmemark-325">[320]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quietus1_326-1"><a href="#cite_note-quietus1-326">[321]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perpetua_327-1"><a href="#cite_note-perpetua-327">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[323]</a></sup> Post punk artists that attained prominence in the 2010s and early 2020s from other countries besides the UK included <a href="/wiki/Parquet_Courts" title="Parquet Courts">Parquet Courts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protomartyr_(band)" title="Protomartyr (band)">Protomartyr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geese_(band)" title="Geese (band)">Geese</a> (United States), <a href="/wiki/Preoccupations" title="Preoccupations">Preoccupations</a> (Canada), <a href="/wiki/Iceage" title="Iceage">Iceage</a> (Denmark), and <a href="/wiki/Viagra_Boys" title="Viagra Boys">Viagra Boys</a> (Sweden).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[324]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[326]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_impact">Social impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Social impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_effects_of_rock_music" title="Social effects of rock music">Social effects of rock music</a></div>
<p>Different subgenres of rock were adopted by, and became central to, the identity of a large number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-culture" title="Sub-culture">sub-cultures</a>. In the 1950s and 1960s, respectively, British youths adopted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teddy_Boys" title="Teddy Boys">Teddy Boy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocker_(subculture)" title="Rocker (subculture)">Rocker</a> subcultures, which revolved around US rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake;_1990;_73-9_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brake;_1990;_73-9-332">[327]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture of the 1960s</a> was closely associated with <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake;_1990;_73-9_332-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brake;_1990;_73-9-332">[327]</a></sup> The mid-late 1970s <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punk subculture</a> began in the US, but it was given a distinctive look by British designer <a href="/wiki/Vivienne_Westwood" title="Vivienne Westwood">Vivienne Westwood</a>, a look which spread worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[328]</a></sup> Out of the punk scene, the <a href="/wiki/Goth_subculture" title="Goth subculture">Goth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emo" title="Emo">Emo</a> subcultures grew, both of which presented distinctive visual styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[329]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG"><img alt="A color photograph showing people from the 1969 Woodstock Festival sitting on grass, in the foreground a back and a white male look at each other" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG/220px-Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG/330px-Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG/440px-Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woodstock_redmond_hair.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1969 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodstock_Festival" title="Woodstock Festival">Woodstock Festival</a> was seen as a celebration of the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture" title="Counterculture">countercultural</a> lifestyle.</div></div></div>
<p>When an international rock culture developed, it supplanted cinema as the major sources of fashion influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruzzi_et_al;_2000;_260_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruzzi_et_al;_2000;_260-335">[330]</a></sup> Paradoxically, followers of rock music have often mistrusted the world of fashion, which has been seen as elevating image above substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruzzi_et_al;_2000;_260_335-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bruzzi_et_al;_2000;_260-335">[330]</a></sup> Rock fashions have been seen as combining elements of different cultures and periods, as well as expressing divergent views on sexuality and gender, and rock music in general has been noted and criticised for facilitating greater sexual freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruzzi_et_al;_2000;_260_335-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bruzzi_et_al;_2000;_260-335">[330]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[331]</a></sup> Rock has also been associated with various forms of drug use, including the <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamines</a> taken by mods in the early to mid-1960s, through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD">LSD</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mescaline" title="Mescaline">mescaline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hashish" title="Hashish">hashish</a> and other hallucinogenic drugs linked with <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">psychedelic rock</a> in the mid-late 1960s and early 1970s; and sometimes to <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> and heroin, all of which have been eulogised in song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[333]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rock has been credited with changing attitudes to race by opening up <a href="/wiki/African-American_culture" title="African-American culture">African-American culture</a> to white audiences; but at the same time, rock has been accused of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_appropriation" title="Cultural appropriation">appropriating</a> and exploiting that culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[335]</a></sup> While rock music has absorbed many influences and introduced Western audiences to different musical traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[336]</a></sup> the global spread of rock music has been interpreted as a form of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_imperialism" title="Cultural imperialism">cultural imperialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[337]</a></sup> Rock music inherited the folk tradition of <a href="/wiki/Protest_song" title="Protest song">protest song</a>, making political statements on subjects such as war, religion, poverty, civil rights, justice and the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[338]</a></sup> Political activism reached a mainstream peak with the "<a href="/wiki/Do_They_Know_It%27s_Christmas%3F" title="Do They Know It's Christmas?">Do They Know It's Christmas?</a>" single (1984) and <a href="/wiki/Live_Aid" title="Live Aid">Live Aid</a> concert for Ethiopia in 1985, which, while successfully raising awareness of world poverty and funds for aid, have also been criticised (along with similar events), for providing a stage for self-aggrandisement and increased profits for the rock stars involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[339]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since its early development, rock music has been associated with rebellion against social and political norms, most obviously in early rock and roll's rejection of an adult-dominated culture, the counterculture's rejection of consumerism and conformity and punk's rejection of all forms of social convention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[340]</a></sup> however, it can also be seen as providing a means of commercial exploitation of such ideas and of diverting youth away from political action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[341]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[342]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_women">Role of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Role of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_music#Popular_music" title="Women in music">Women in music § Popular music</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women_in_heavy_metal" title="Women in heavy metal">Women in heavy metal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="748" data-file-width="528" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg/170px-Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg/255px-Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg/340px-Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suzi_Quatro_plays_a_bass_guitar_while_she_sings_at_AIS_Arena.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Suzi_Quatro" title="Suzi Quatro">Suzi Quatro</a> is a singer, bassist and bandleader. When she launched her career in 1973, she was one of the few prominent women instrumentalists and bandleaders.</div></div></div>
<p>Professional women instrumentalists are uncommon in rock genres such as heavy metal although bands such as <a href="/wiki/Within_Temptation" title="Within Temptation">Within Temptation</a> have featured women as lead singers with men playing instruments. According to Schaap and Berkers, "playing in a band is largely a male homosocial activity, that is, learning to play in a band is largely a peer-based ... experience, shaped by existing sex-segregated friendship networks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[343]</a></sup> They note that rock music "is often defined as a form of male rebellion vis-à-vis female bedroom culture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p102_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p102-349">[344]</a></sup> (The theory of "bedroom culture" argues that society influences girls to not engage in crime and deviance by virtually trapping them in their bedroom; it was developed by a sociologist named <a href="/wiki/Angela_McRobbie" title="Angela McRobbie">Angela McRobbie</a>.) In popular music, there has been a gendered "distinction between public (male) and private (female) participation" in music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p102_349-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p102-349">[344]</a></sup> "Several scholars have argued that men exclude women from bands or from the bands' rehearsals, recordings, performances, and other social activities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p104_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p104-350">[345]</a></sup> "Women are mainly regarded as passive and private consumers of allegedly slick, prefabricated – hence, inferior – pop music ..., excluding them from participating as high status rock musicians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p104_350-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p104-350">[345]</a></sup> One of the reasons that there are rarely mixed gender bands is that "bands operate as tight-knit units in which homosocial solidarity – social bonds between people of the same sex ...  – plays a crucial role".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchaapandBerkers2014p104_350-2"><a href="#cite_note-SchaapandBerkers2014p104-350">[345]</a></sup> In the 1960s rock music scene, "singing was sometimes an acceptable pastime for a girl, but playing an instrument ... simply wasn't done".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rebeatmag.com_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-rebeatmag.com-351">[346]</a></sup>
</p><p>"The rebellion of rock music was largely a male rebellion; the women – often, in the 1950s and '60s, girls in their teens – in rock usually sang songs as personæ utterly dependent on their macho boyfriends ...". Philip Auslander says that "Although there were many women in rock by the late 1960s, most performed only as singers, a traditionally feminine position in popular music". Though some women played instruments in American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-female_bands" title="All-female bands">all-female garage rock bands</a>, none of these bands achieved more than regional success. So they "did not provide viable templates for women's on-going participation in rock".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2004_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2004-352">[347]</a></sup> In relation to the gender composition of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">heavy metal bands</a>, it has been said that "[h]eavy metal performers are almost exclusively male"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake_1990_87–91_353-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brake_1990_87–91-353">[348]</a></sup> "...at least until the mid-1980s"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[349]</a></sup> apart from "...exceptions such as <a href="/wiki/Girlschool" title="Girlschool">Girlschool</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brake_1990_87–91_353-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brake_1990_87–91-353">[348]</a></sup> However, "...now [in the 2010s] maybe more than ever–strong metal women have put up their dukes and got down to it",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[350]</a></sup> "carv[ing] out a considerable place for [them]selves."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[351]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Suzi_Quatro" title="Suzi Quatro">Suzi Quatro</a> emerged in 1973, "no other prominent female musician worked in rock simultaneously as a singer, instrumentalist, songwriter, and bandleader".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2004_352-1"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2004-352">[347]</a></sup> According to Auslander, she was "kicking down the male door in rock and roll and proving that a female <i>musician</i> ... and this is a point I am extremely concerned about ... could play as well if not better than the boys".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Auslander2004_352-2"><a href="#cite_note-Auslander2004-352">[347]</a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/All-female_band" title="All-female band">all-female band</a> is a musical group in genres such as rock and blues which is exclusively composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_musician" title="Female musician">female musicians</a>. This is distinct from a girl group, in which the female members are solely vocalists, though this terminology is not universally followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[352]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><span><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Guitar_%281867%29_-_The_Noun_Project.svg/27px-Guitar_%281867%29_-_The_Noun_Project.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Guitar_%281867%29_-_The_Noun_Project.svg/41px-Guitar_%281867%29_-_The_Noun_Project.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Guitar_%281867%29_-_The_Noun_Project.svg/55px-Guitar_%281867%29_-_The_Noun_Project.svg.png 2x" width="27"/></span><span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Rock_music" title="Portal:Rock music">Rock music portal</a></span></li></ul></div>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The terms "pop-rock" and "power pop" have been used to describe more commercially successful music that uses elements from, or the form of, rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Pop-rock has been defined as an "upbeat variety of rock music represented by artists such as Elton John, <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Everly_Brothers" title="The Everly Brothers">the Everly Brothers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rod_Stewart" title="Rod Stewart">Rod Stewart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago_(band)" title="Chicago (band)">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Frampton" title="Peter Frampton">Peter Frampton</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The term <i>power pop</i> was coined by <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">Pete Townshend</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">the Who</a> in 1966, but not much used until it was applied to bands like <a href="/wiki/Badfinger" title="Badfinger">Badfinger</a> in the 1970s, who proved some of the most commercially successful of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Having died down in the late 1950s, doo wop enjoyed a revival in the same period, with hits for acts like <a href="/wiki/The_Marcels" title="The Marcels">the Marcels</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Capris" title="The Capris">the Capris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Williams_and_the_Zodiacs" title="Maurice Williams and the Zodiacs">Maurice Williams and the Zodiacs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shep_and_the_Limelights" title="Shep and the Limelights">Shep and the Limelights</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002DooWop_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002DooWop-42">[42]</a></sup> The rise of girl groups like <a href="/wiki/The_Chantels" title="The Chantels">the Chantels</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Shirelles" title="The Shirelles">the Shirelles</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Crystals" title="The Crystals">the Crystals</a> placed an emphasis on harmonies and polished production that was in contrast to earlier rock and roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Some of the most significant girl group hits were products of the <a href="/wiki/Brill_Building" title="Brill Building">Brill Building</a> Sound, named after the block in New York where many songwriters were based, which included the number 1 hit for the Shirelles "<a href="/wiki/Will_You_Love_Me_Tomorrow" title="Will You Love Me Tomorrow">Will You Love Me Tomorrow</a>" in 1960, penned by the partnership of <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Goffin" title="Gerry Goffin">Gerry Goffin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carole_King" title="Carole King">Carole King</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Brill_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Brill-62">[61]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All of these elements, including the close harmonies of doo-wop and girl groups, the carefully crafted songwriting of the <a href="/wiki/Brill_Building_(genre)" title="Brill Building (genre)">Brill Building Sound</a> and the polished production values of soul, have been seen as influencing the <a href="/wiki/Beat_music" title="Beat music">Merseybeat</a> sound, particularly the early work of <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>, and through them the form of later rock music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Mersey_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Mersey-69">[67]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Only the Beach Boys were able to sustain a creative career into the mid-1960s, producing a string of hit singles and albums, including the highly regarded <i><a href="/wiki/Pet_Sounds" title="Pet Sounds">Pet Sounds</a></i> in 1966, which made them, arguably, the only American rock or pop act that could rival the Beatles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogdanov2002Beachboys_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bogdanov2002Beachboys-74">[71]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Detroit, garage rock's legacy remained alive into the early 1970s, with bands such as the <a href="/wiki/MC5" title="MC5">MC5</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Stooges" title="The Stooges">the Stooges</a>, who employed a much more aggressive approach to the form. These bands began to be labelled <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk rock</a> and are now often seen as <a href="/wiki/Proto-punk" title="Proto-punk">proto-punk</a> or proto-<a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">hard rock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G._Thompson;_2007;_134_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-G._Thompson;_2007;_134-103">[99]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rock_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-CG2-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-CG2_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFChristgau1981"><a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Christgau, Robert</a> (1981). <a class="external text" href="https://www.robertchristgau.com/get_album.php?id=6904" rel="nofollow">"Genesis: Selling England by the Pound"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Christgau%27s_Record_Guide:_Rock_Albums_of_the_Seventies" title="Christgau's Record Guide: Rock Albums of the Seventies">Christgau's Record Guide: Rock Albums of the Seventies</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ticknor_%26_Fields" title="Ticknor &amp; Fields">Ticknor &amp; Fields</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-89919-025-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-89919-025-1"><bdi>0-89919-025-1</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-CG-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-CG_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-CG_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-CG_17-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFChristgau1981"><a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Christgau, Robert</a> (1981). <a class="external text" href="https://www.robertchristgau.com/xg/bk-cg70/decade.php" rel="nofollow">"The Decade"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Christgau%27s_Record_Guide:_Rock_Albums_of_the_Seventies" title="Christgau's Record Guide: Rock Albums of the Seventies">Christgau's Record Guide: Rock Albums of the Seventies</a></i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ticknor_%26_Fields" title="Ticknor &amp; Fields">Ticknor &amp; Fields</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-89919-025-1" title="Special:BookSources/0-89919-025-1"><bdi>0-89919-025-1</bdi></a><span class="reference-accessdate">.
